<p>The <b>Australian Greens</b> (commonly known as <b>The Greens</b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Green_politics" title="Green politics">green</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_political_parties_in_Australia" title="List of political parties in Australia">political party in Australia</a>.
</p><p>The party was formed in 1992 and is a confederation of eight state and territorial parties. In addition to <a href="/wiki/Environmentalism" title="Environmentalism">environmentalism</a>, the party cites four core values: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecological_sustainability" title="Ecological sustainability">ecological sustainability</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social_justice" title="Social justice">social justice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grassroots_democracy" title="Grassroots democracy">grassroots democracy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peace_movement" title="Peace movement">peace and non-violence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-themonthly.com.au_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-themonthly.com.au-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Party constituencies can be traced to various origins – notably the early <a href="/wiki/Environmental_movement_in_Australia" title="Environmental movement in Australia">environmental movement in Australia</a> and the formation of the <a href="/wiki/United_Tasmania_Group" title="United Tasmania Group">United Tasmania Group</a> (UTG), one of the first <a href="/wiki/Green_party" title="Green party">green parties</a> in the world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> but also the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_disarmament" title="Nuclear disarmament">nuclear disarmament</a> movement in Western Australia and sections of the industrial left in New South Wales. Co-ordination between environmentalist groups occurred in the 1980s with <a href="#Origins">various significant protests</a>. Key people involved in these campaigns included <a href="/wiki/Bob_Brown" title="Bob Brown">Bob Brown</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christine_Milne" title="Christine Milne">Christine Milne</a> who went on to contest and win seats in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tasmanian_Parliament" title="Tasmanian Parliament">Tasmanian Parliament</a> and eventually form the <a href="/wiki/Tasmanian_Greens" title="Tasmanian Greens">Tasmanian Greens</a>; both Brown and Milne subsequently became leaders of the federal party.
</p><p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2016" title="Australian federal election, 2016">2016 federal election</a>, the Australian Greens have nine <a href="/wiki/Australian_Senate" title="Australian Senate">senators</a> and one member in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/House_of_Representatives_(Australia)" title="House of Representatives (Australia)">lower house</a>, 23 elected representatives across state and territory parliaments, more than 100 local councillors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gc40_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-gc40-5">[5]</a></sup> and over 15,000 party members (as of 2016).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbog_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbog-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Formation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The formation of the Australian Greens in 1992 brought together over a dozen green groups, from state and local organisations, some of which had existed for 20 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-themonthly.com.au_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-themonthly.com.au-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>The precursor to the Tasmanian Greens (the earliest existent member of the federation of parties that is the Australian Greens), the <a href="/wiki/United_Tasmania_Group" title="United Tasmania Group">United Tasmania Group</a>, was founded in 1972 to oppose the construction of new dams to flood <a href="/wiki/Lake_Pedder" title="Lake Pedder">Lake Pedder</a>. The campaign failed to prevent the flooding of Lake Pedder and the party failed to gain political representation. One of the party's candidates was <a href="/wiki/Bob_Brown" title="Bob Brown">Bob Brown</a>, then a doctor in <a href="/wiki/Launceston,_Tasmania" title="Launceston, Tasmania">Launceston</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the late 1970s and 1980s, a public campaign to prevent the construction of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franklin_Dam" title="Franklin Dam">Franklin Dam</a> in Tasmania saw environmentalist and activist <a href="/wiki/Norm_Sanders" title="Norm Sanders">Norm Sanders</a> elected to the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Tasmania" title="Parliament of Tasmania">Tasmanian Parliament</a> as an <a href="/wiki/Australian_Democrats" title="Australian Democrats">Australian Democrat</a>. Brown, then director of the <a href="/wiki/Tasmanian_Wilderness_Society" title="Tasmanian Wilderness Society">Wilderness Society</a>, contested the election as an independent, but failed to win a seat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1982 Norm Sanders resigned from Parliament, and Brown was elected to replace him in a countback.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>During her 1984 visit to Australia, <a href="/wiki/Alliance_90/The_Greens" title="Alliance 90/The Greens">West German Greens</a> parliamentarian <a href="/wiki/Petra_Kelly" title="Petra Kelly">Petra Kelly</a> urged that the various Greens groups in Australia develop a national identity. Partly as a result of this, 50 Greens activists gathered in Tasmania in December to organise a national conference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>The title "The Greens" had been first registered in Sydney in the 1980s by what <i>The Monthly Magazine</i> described as "a band of inner-city radicals committed to resident action, nuclear disarmament and urban environmental causes, such as stopping expressways and preserving parklands". The group formed as the Sydney Greens and evolved into the Green Alliance, with the stated aim of not forming a "traditional hierarchy party". According to party co-ordinator <a href="/wiki/Hall_Greenland" title="Hall Greenland">Hall Greenland</a>, when amalgamation with Bob Brown's Tasmanian movement was first mooted, Brown was hesitant owing to what he perceived as the "anarchic leftism" of the Sydney movement. <a href="/wiki/Greens_New_South_Wales" title="Greens New South Wales">The Greens NSW</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greens_Western_Australia" title="Greens Western Australia">The Greens (WA)</a> were also wary of amalgamation owing to local autonomy concerns and a 1986 attempt by Brown to form a national party failed. The movement for a national party continued however. In an effort to reduce the influence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Democratic_Socialist_Party_(Australia)" title="Democratic Socialist Party (Australia)">Democratic Socialist Party</a> (formerly Socialist Workers Party) in The Greens NSW, Brown successfully moved for a ban on dual party membership by Greens. Following formation of the national party in 1992, regional emphasis variations remained within the Greens, with members of the "industrial left" remaining a presence in the New South Wales branch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-themonthly.com.au_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-themonthly.com.au-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Green movement gained its first federal parliamentary representative when Senator <a href="/wiki/Jo_Vallentine" title="Jo Vallentine">Jo Vallentine</a> of Western Australia, who had been elected in 1984 for the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Disarmament_Party" title="Nuclear Disarmament Party">Nuclear Disarmament Party</a> and later sat as an independent, was part of the formation of and joined The Greens (WA), a party formed in Western Australia, and not affiliated to the Australian Greens at that time.
</p><p>In 1992, representatives from around the nation gathered in North Sydney and agreed to form the Australian Greens, although the state Greens parties, particularly in Western Australia, retained their separate identities for a period. Brown resigned from the Tasmanian Parliament in 1993, and in 1996 he was elected as a senator for Tasmania, the first elected as an Australian Greens candidate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Initially the most successful Greens group during this period was The Greens (WA), at that time still a separate organisation from the Australian Greens. Vallentine was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Christabel_Chamarette" title="Christabel Chamarette">Christabel Chamarette</a> in 1992, and she was joined by <a href="/wiki/Dee_Margetts" title="Dee Margetts">Dee Margetts</a> in 1993. But Chamarette was defeated in the <a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_1996" title="Australian federal election, 1996">1996 federal election</a>. Margetts opposed the industrial relations reform agenda of the <a href="/wiki/Howard_Government" title="Howard Government">Howard Government</a>. Following the 'Cavalcade to Canberra' protest of 19 August 1996, in which 2000 breakaway civilians <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1996_Parliament_House_Riot" title="1996 Parliament House Riot">rioted in and around Parliament House</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Margetts told the Senate that "The Greens (WA) do not associate ourselves with the violent action" and that while "there are obviously some in the Greens movement who have differing opinions about that" she personally did not think there was "any justification for the use of violence to the extent that we saw".  Margetts lost her seat in the <a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_1998" title="Australian federal election, 1998">1998 federal election</a>, leaving Brown as the sole Australian Greens senator.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leadership_of_Bob_Brown">Leadership of Bob Brown</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Leadership of Bob Brown">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Frontbench_of_Bob_Brown" title="Frontbench of Bob Brown">Frontbench of Bob Brown</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bob_Brown_at_2008_climate_change_rally_DSC_6368.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Bob_Brown_at_2008_climate_change_rally_DSC_6368.JPG/220px-Bob_Brown_at_2008_climate_change_rally_DSC_6368.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Bob_Brown_at_2008_climate_change_rally_DSC_6368.JPG/330px-Bob_Brown_at_2008_climate_change_rally_DSC_6368.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Bob_Brown_at_2008_climate_change_rally_DSC_6368.JPG/440px-Bob_Brown_at_2008_climate_change_rally_DSC_6368.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bob_Brown_at_2008_climate_change_rally_DSC_6368.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bob Brown at a <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> rally in Melbourne on 5 July 2008</div></div></div>
<p>The national party initially resisted appointing a party leader, however <a href="/wiki/Bob_Brown" title="Bob Brown">Bob Brown</a> was later selected. The New South Wales Greens remained ideologically opposed to appointing a leader and continue not to appoint such a position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-themonthly.com.au_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-themonthly.com.au-3">[3]</a></sup> The WA Greens had lost office in the Senate by 1998, leaving Bob Brown as the sole representative of the party. Thereafter, the national vote was set to increase consecutively at elections up until 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-themonthly.com.au_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-themonthly.com.au-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (September 2013)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2001_federal_election_onward">2001 federal election onward</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 2001 federal election onward">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2001" title="Australian federal election, 2001">2001 federal election</a>, Brown was re-elected as a senator for Tasmania, and a second Greens senator, <a href="/wiki/Kerry_Nettle" title="Kerry Nettle">Kerry Nettle</a>, was elected in New South Wales. The Greens opposed the <a href="/wiki/Howard_Government" title="Howard Government">Howard Government</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Solution" title="Pacific Solution">Pacific Solution</a> of offshore processing for asylum seekers, and opposed the bipartisan offers of support to the <a href="/wiki/ANZUS" title="ANZUS">US alliance</a> and <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001–present)">Afghanistan War</a> by the government and <a href="/wiki/Kim_Beazley" title="Kim Beazley">Beazley Opposition</a> in the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">11 September terrorist attacks</a> in 2001, describing the Afghanistan commitment as "warmongering".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> This contributed to increased support for the Greens by disaffected Labor Party voters and helped identify the Greens as more than just a single-issue environmental party. On 19 October 2002 the Greens won a <a href="/wiki/Australian_House_of_Representatives" title="Australian House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a> seat for the first time when <a href="/wiki/Michael_Organ" title="Michael Organ">Michael Organ</a> won the <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Cunningham" title="Division of Cunningham">Cunningham</a> <a href="/wiki/Cunningham_by-election,_2002" title="Cunningham by-election, 2002">by-election</a>.
</p><p>In the lead-up to the <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq War</a>, in September 2002, Bob Brown said that the Greens would oppose military action in Iraq regardless of the position of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">United Nations Security Council</a> and said that any conflict would be "a vengeance for the S11 attack that's involved here as well as the American corporations wanting to get their hands on the Iraqi oil" and that if <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">Saddam Hussein</a> "does have weapons of mass destruction, the attack might be the thing that gets him to use them", so it would be better to "resolv[e] the Palestinian crisis, which could lead—open up a real avenue to peace in the Middle East, and neutralise Saddam Hussein by doing it".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2004_federal_election_onward">2004 federal election onward</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 2004 federal election onward">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2004" title="Australian federal election, 2004">2004 federal election</a>, the Greens' primary vote rose by 2.3% to 7.2%. This won them two additional Senate seats, taken by <a href="/wiki/Christine_Milne" title="Christine Milne">Christine Milne</a> in Tasmania and <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Siewert" title="Rachel Siewert">Rachel Siewert</a> in Western Australia, bringing the total to four. However, the success of the Howard Government in winning a majority in the Senate meant that the Greens' influence on legislation decreased. <a href="/wiki/Michael_Organ" title="Michael Organ">Michael Organ</a> was defeated by the Labor Party candidate in Cunningham.
</p><p>Additionally, in the 2004 election there was an intense media campaign from the socially conservative <a href="/wiki/Family_First_Party" title="Family First Party">Family First Party</a>, including a television advertisement labelling the Greens the "Extreme Greens". Competitive preferencing strategies prompted by the nature of Senate balloting (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Australian_electoral_system" title="Australian electoral system">Australian electoral system</a>) lead to the Australian Labor Party and the Democrats ranking Family First higher than the Greens on their Senate tickets, and the Greens losing preferences they would normally have received from the two parties. Consequently, although outpolling Family First by a ratio of more than four to one first-preference votes, Victorian Family First candidate <a href="/wiki/Steve_Fielding" title="Steve Fielding">Steve Fielding</a> was elected on preferences over the Australian Greens' <a href="/wiki/David_Risstrom" title="David Risstrom">David Risstrom</a>, an unintended consequence of these strategies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> In Tasmania, Christine Milne only narrowly gained her Senate seat before a Family First candidate, despite obtaining almost the full required quota of primary votes. It was only the high incidence of "below-the-line" voting in Tasmania that negated the effect of the preference-swap deal between Labor and Family First.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Australian Greens fielded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2004_Australian_Greens_candidates" title="2004 Australian Greens candidates">candidates</a> in every House of Representatives seat in Australia, and for all state and territory Senate positions. The Greens (WA) were able to win <a href="/wiki/Members_of_the_Western_Australian_Legislative_Council,_2001%E2%80%932005" title="Members of the Western Australian Legislative Council, 2001–2005">Legislative Council</a> seats in rural and remote-area seats (Mining and Pastoral, Agricultural and South West provinces).
</p><p>In 2005, the Greens' <a href="/wiki/Lee_Rhiannon" title="Lee Rhiannon">Lee Rhiannon</a> lobbied the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Vatican</a> to reject Australian Cardinal <a href="/wiki/George_Pell" title="George Pell">George Pell</a> as a candidate for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papacy" title="Papacy">Papacy</a> on the basis of his support for conservative Catholic moral doctrine. In 2007, Rhiannon referred remarks made by Pell opposing <a href="/wiki/Embryonic_stem_cell" title="Embryonic stem cell">embryonic stem cell</a> research to the New South Wales parliamentary privileges committee for allegedly being in "contempt of parliament". Pell was cleared of the charge and described the move as a "clumsy attempt to curb religious freedom and freedom of speech".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Australian Greens' primary vote increased by 4.1% in the <a href="/wiki/South_Australian_state_election,_2006" title="South Australian state election, 2006">2006 South Australia election</a>, 1.% in the <a href="/wiki/Queensland_state_election,_2006" title="Queensland state election, 2006">2006 Queensland election</a>, and 0.7% in the <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales_state_election,_2007" title="New South Wales state election, 2007">2007 election in New South Wales</a>.
</p><p>The results for the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_state_election,_2006" title="Victorian state election, 2006">2006 Victoria state election</a> were mixed, with an improved vote for the Greens in the lower house, but a fall in their upper-house vote.
</p><p>Against this upward trend was a swing of 1.5% away from the Greens in the <a href="/wiki/Tasmanian_state_election,_2006#Results" title="Tasmanian state election, 2006">2006 Tasmania state election</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 31 August 2004, the Melbourne newspaper the <i><a href="/wiki/Herald_Sun" title="Herald Sun">Herald Sun</a></i> published a page-three-story by journalist <a href="/wiki/Gerard_McManus" title="Gerard McManus">Gerard McManus</a> entitled "Greens back illegal drugs" in the lead-up to the <a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2004" title="Australian federal election, 2004">2004 federal election</a>.  In response to the article, Brown lodged a complaint with the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Press_Council" title="Australian Press Council">Australian Press Council</a>. After the election, the Press Council upheld Brown's complaint. An appeal by the <i>Herald Sun</i> was dismissed and it was ordered to publish the Press Council's adjudication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2007_federal_election_onward">2007 federal election onward</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 2007 federal election onward">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BobBrownSpeaking.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="634" data-file-width="950" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/BobBrownSpeaking.jpg/220px-BobBrownSpeaking.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/BobBrownSpeaking.jpg/330px-BobBrownSpeaking.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/BobBrownSpeaking.jpg/440px-BobBrownSpeaking.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BobBrownSpeaking.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bob Brown lays out the Greens' climate change policies in the lead-up to the <a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2007" title="Australian federal election, 2007">2007 federal election</a></div></div></div>
<p>As in previous years, the Greens' vote was strongest in inner-city seats, including <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Melbourne" title="Division of Melbourne">Melbourne</a> (22.7% of primary votes), <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Sydney" title="Division of Sydney">Sydney</a> (20.7%), <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Grayndler" title="Division of Grayndler">Grayndler</a> (18.7%), <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Denison" title="Division of Denison">Denison</a> (18.6%) and <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Batman" title="Division of Batman">Batman</a> (17.2%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Strong votes were also recorded in <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_of_Australia" title="Liberal Party of Australia">Liberal</a>-held city based seats such as <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Higgins" title="Division of Higgins">Higgins</a> (10.8%), <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Kooyong" title="Division of Kooyong">Kooyong</a> (11.8%) <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Curtin" title="Division of Curtin">Curtin</a> (13.4%) and <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Wentworth" title="Division of Wentworth">Wentworth</a> (15.0%). The primary vote for the Greens in suburban and regional areas was generally smaller.
</p><p>The Greens directly contributed to Howard's defeat in his own electorate, the Sydney-area seat of <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Bennelong" title="Division of Bennelong">Bennelong</a>.  Greens candidate Lindsay Peters received 5.5% of the primary vote.  He was eliminated after the 14th count, and more than three-fourths of his preferences went to Labor challenger <a href="/wiki/Maxine_McKew" title="Maxine McKew">Maxine McKew</a>.  This margin was enough to make McKew only the second person to unseat a sitting prime minister.
</p><p>The Greens increased their national vote by 1.38 points to 9.04% at the <a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2007" title="Australian federal election, 2007">2007 federal election</a>, with a net increase of one senator to a total of five. Senators <a href="/wiki/Bob_Brown" title="Bob Brown">Bob Brown</a> (Tas) and <a href="/wiki/Kerry_Nettle" title="Kerry Nettle">Kerry Nettle</a> (NSW) were up for re-election, Brown was re-elected, but Nettle was unsuccessful, becoming the only Australian Greens senator to lose their seat. Elected at the <a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2001" title="Australian federal election, 2001">2001 federal election</a> on a primary vote of 4.36% in New South Wales with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/One_Nation_(Australia)" title="One Nation (Australia)">One Nation</a> and micro-party preference flows,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> she failed to gain re-election in 2007 due to preferences, despite an increase in the New South Wales Green primary vote to 8.43%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greenvotes.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="620" data-file-width="927" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Greenvotes.PNG/300px-Greenvotes.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Greenvotes.PNG/450px-Greenvotes.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Greenvotes.PNG/600px-Greenvotes.PNG 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greenvotes.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Percentage of lower house first preference Green votes at the <a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2007" title="Australian federal election, 2007">2007 federal election</a> in selected Australian cities</div></div></div>
<p>Other Greens Senate candidates were <a href="/wiki/Larissa_Waters" title="Larissa Waters">Larissa Waters</a> (Qld), <a href="/wiki/Richard_Di_Natale" title="Richard Di Natale">Richard Di Natale</a> (Vic), <a href="/wiki/Scott_Ludlam" title="Scott Ludlam">Scott Ludlam</a> (WA), <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Hanson-Young" title="Sarah Hanson-Young">Sarah Hanson-Young</a> (SA) and <a href="/wiki/Kerrie_Tucker" title="Kerrie Tucker">Kerrie Tucker</a> (ACT). Ludlam and Hanson-Young were elected and took up office on 26 August 2008 when all senators elected on 24 November 2007 were sworn in.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>This was also the first general election for the Greens in which a lower house seat went "maverick". In the <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Melbourne" title="Division of Melbourne">Division of Melbourne</a>, the Greens polled 22.80% of the primary vote, overtaking the Liberals on preferences, finishing on a <a href="/wiki/Two-party-preferred_vote" title="Two-party-preferred vote">two-party-preferred</a> figure of 45.29% against Labor.
</p><p>An extensive campaign was undertaken in the ACT, in an attempt to end coalition control of the Senate immediately after the election, as territory senators take their place at this time as opposed to their state counterparts on the next 1 July. The ACT elects two seats with terms (in parallel with those of the House of Representatives), so a larger quota than normal is required for election. Despite a swing of 5.1 points to the Greens, on 21.5%, their best result in any state or territory, the party <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Senate_results_for_the_Australian_federal_election,_2007#Australian_Capital_Territory" title="Senate results for the Australian federal election, 2007">fell significantly short</a> of the required quota.
</p><p>At the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Territory_general_election,_2008" title="Northern Territory general election, 2008">2008 Northern Territory election</a>, the Greens ran in six of the 25 seats in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unicameral" title="Unicameral">unicameral</a> parliament, averaging 16% of the vote but won no seats. At the <a href="/wiki/Western_Australian_state_election,_2008" title="Western Australian state election, 2008">2008 Western Australian election</a>, the Greens won 11–12 percent of the statewide vote in both the lower and upper houses, with four of 36 seats in the latter, an increase of two.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Capital_Territory_general_election,_2008" title="Australian Capital Territory general election, 2008">2008 Australian Capital Territory election</a>, conducted under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hare-Clark_system" title="Hare-Clark system">Hare-Clark system</a> of <a href="/wiki/Proportional_representation" title="Proportional representation">proportional representation</a>, the Greens doubled their vote to around 15%, going from one to four seats in the 17-member <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unicameral" title="Unicameral">unicameral</a> parliament, giving them the <a href="/wiki/Balance_of_power_(parliament)" title="Balance of power (parliament)">balance of power</a>. After almost two weeks of deliberations, the Greens chose to allow Labor to form a <a href="/wiki/Minority_government" title="Minority government">minority government</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The Greens held the post of Speaker in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ACT_Legislative_Assembly" title="ACT Legislative Assembly">ACT Legislative Assembly</a>, the first for a Green party in Australia.
</p><p>In November 2008, Senator <a href="/wiki/Christine_Milne" title="Christine Milne">Christine Milne</a> was elected deputy leader in a ballot contested against Senator <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Siewert" title="Rachel Siewert">Rachel Siewert</a>.
</p><p>In May 2009, the Greens won their second-ever single-member electorate, with <a href="/wiki/Adele_Carles" title="Adele Carles">Adele Carles</a> winning the <a href="/wiki/Fremantle_state_by-election,_2009" title="Fremantle state by-election, 2009">Fremantle by-election</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Western_Australian_Legislative_Assembly" title="Western Australian Legislative Assembly">Western Australian Legislative Assembly</a>. The seat had been held by the Labor Party since 1924.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> It was the first time the Greens had outpolled the Labor Party on the primary vote in any Labor-held seat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2009, the Greens received over 30 percent of the primary vote in the federal <a href="/wiki/Higgins_by-election,_2009" title="Higgins by-election, 2009">Higgins by-election</a> in Victoria, in the absence of a Labor candidate. It is the highest primary vote recorded by the Greens in a Liberal-held lower-house seat.
</p><p>At the <a href="/wiki/Tasmanian_state_election,_2010" title="Tasmanian state election, 2010">March 2010 Tasmanian state election</a>, the Greens won 21.6 percent of the primary vote amongst the five multi-member electorates, resulting in the Greens winning five of twenty-five seats in the <a href="/wiki/Tasmanian_House_of_Assembly" title="Tasmanian House of Assembly">lower house</a> and holding the balance of power. With Labor and the Liberals winning ten seats each, the Greens backed a Labor minority government. Tasmanian Greens Leader <a href="/wiki/Nick_McKim" title="Nick McKim">Nick McKim</a> was appointed to the new Labor-Green cabinet, making him the first Green Minister in Australia.
</p><p>In the lead-up to the <a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2010" title="Australian federal election, 2010">2010 Australian federal election</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Christian_Lobby" title="Australian Christian Lobby">Australian Christian Lobby</a> and the Catholic Archbishop of Sydney criticised Greens policies as "anti-Christian". In an 8 August opinion article for Sydney's <i>Sunday Telegraph</i> newspaper, Cardinal Archbishop <a href="/wiki/George_Pell" title="George Pell">George Pell</a> wrote that the Greens were hostile to the family, opposed to religious schools, had pressured against Catholic management of Calvary Hospital in Canberra and said the party contained <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stalinist" title="Stalinist">Stalinists</a> and a wing who were "watermelons" -"green on the outside, red on the inside" whose policies were  "impractical and expensive, which will not help the poor".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> In response to the article, Senator Bob Brown said Pell was "bearing false witness" and that the Greens were in fact "much closer to mainstream Christian thinking than Cardinal Pell".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesuit" title="Jesuit">Jesuit</a> human rights lawyer Fr. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frank_Brennan_(Jesuit)" title="Frank Brennan (Jesuit)">Frank Brennan</a> responded in an essay by saying that while some Greens might be anti-Christian, others like <a href="/wiki/Lin_Hatfield_Dodds" title="Lin Hatfield Dodds">Lin Hatfield Dodds</a> "have given distinguished public service in their churches for decades." On some policy issues, wrote Brennan, "the Greens have a more Christian message than the major parties", while on issues such as abortion, stem-cell research, same-sex marriage and funding for church schools, the party would never be able to "carry the day given that policy changes in these areas will occur only if they are supported by a majority from both major political parties".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the lead up to the 2010 election, Bob Brown opposed the senate pre-selection of high-profile New South Wales Green <a href="/wiki/Lee_Rhiannon" title="Lee Rhiannon">Lee Rhiannon</a> in favour of environmentalist <a href="/wiki/Cate_Faehrmann" title="Cate Faehrmann">Cate Faehrmann</a>, saying that the Greens needed "new blood". Rhiannon, a socialist who had also campaigned on gun control, foreign aid, political donations and urban renewal said that there were differing visions for the future of the Greens – one of increased centralisation of party decision making versus a vision she supported of empowering membership. Rhiannon was confirmed as the candidate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-themonthly.com.au_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-themonthly.com.au-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2010_federal_election_onward">2010 federal election onward</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 2010 federal election onward">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table style="float:right; padding:1px 3px; margin:0 0 0 8px; Background:#eee; Border:2px outset #aaa; border-radius:15px;">
<tbody><tr>
<td>
<p><b>Federal Senate election results<br/></b>
(Greens – percent of overall vote)<b></b>
</p>
<center>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_1996" title="Australian federal election, 1996">1996</a>: <span style="visibility:hidden;color:transparent;">0</span>2.4%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_1998" title="Australian federal election, 1998">1998</a>: <span style="visibility:hidden;color:transparent;">0</span>2.7%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2001" title="Australian federal election, 2001">2001</a>: <span style="visibility:hidden;color:transparent;">0</span>4.9%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2004" title="Australian federal election, 2004">2004</a>: <span style="visibility:hidden;color:transparent;">0</span>7.7%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2007" title="Australian federal election, 2007">2007</a>: <span style="visibility:hidden;color:transparent;">0</span>9.0%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2010" title="Australian federal election, 2010">2010</a>: <span style="visibility:hidden;color:transparent;">0</span>13.1%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2013" title="Australian federal election, 2013">2013</a>: <span style="visibility:hidden;color:transparent;">0</span>8.7%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2016" title="Australian federal election, 2016">2016</a>: <span style="visibility:hidden;color:transparent;">0</span>8.7%</li></ul>
</center>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2010" title="Australian federal election, 2010">August 2010 federal election</a> the Greens received a four percent swing to finish with 13 percent of the vote (more than 1.6 million votes) in the Senate, a first for any Australian minor party. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Senate_results_for_the_Australian_federal_election,_2010" title="Senate results for the Australian federal election, 2010">Senate vote throughout the states</a> was between 10 and 20 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VTR_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-VTR-38">[38]</a></sup> The Greens won a seat in each of the six states at the election, again a first for any Australian minor party, bringing the party to a total of nine senators from July 2011, holding the <a href="/wiki/Balance_of_power_(parliament)" title="Balance of power (parliament)">balance of power</a> in the Senate. The new senators were <a href="/wiki/Lee_Rhiannon" title="Lee Rhiannon">Lee Rhiannon</a> in New South Wales, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Di_Natale" title="Richard Di Natale">Richard Di Natale</a> in Victoria, <a href="/wiki/Larissa_Waters" title="Larissa Waters">Larissa Waters</a> in Queensland, <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Siewert" title="Rachel Siewert">Rachel Siewert</a> in Western Australia, <a href="/wiki/Penny_Wright" title="Penny Wright">Penny Wright</a> in South Australia and <a href="/wiki/Christine_Milne" title="Christine Milne">Christine Milne</a> in Tasmania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABC_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-ABC-39">[39]</a></sup> Incumbents <a href="/wiki/Scott_Ludlam" title="Scott Ludlam">Scott Ludlam</a> in Western Australia, <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Hanson-Young" title="Sarah Hanson-Young">Sarah Hanson-Young</a> in South Australia and <a href="/wiki/Bob_Brown" title="Bob Brown">Bob Brown</a> in Tasmania were not due for re-election. The Greens also won their first <a href="/wiki/Australian_House_of_Representatives" title="Australian House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a> seat at a general election, the seat of <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Melbourne" title="Division of Melbourne">Melbourne</a> with candidate <a href="/wiki/Adam_Bandt" title="Adam Bandt">Adam Bandt</a>, who was a <a href="/wiki/Crossbencher" title="Crossbencher">crossbencher</a> in the first <a href="/wiki/Hung_parliament" title="Hung parliament">hung parliament</a> since the <a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_1940" title="Australian federal election, 1940">1940 federal election</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Almost two weeks after the election, Bandt and the Greens agreed to support a <a href="/wiki/Julia_Gillard" title="Julia Gillard">Gillard</a> <a href="/wiki/Australian_Labor_Party" title="Australian Labor Party">Labor</a> <a href="/wiki/Minority_government" title="Minority government">minority government</a> on <a href="/wiki/Confidence_and_supply" title="Confidence and supply">confidence and supply</a> votes. Labor was returned to government with the additional support of three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Independent_(politics)" title="Independent (politics)">independent</a> crossbenchers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greenlaboragreement_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-greenlaboragreement-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>The election resulted in a <a href="/wiki/Hung_parliament" title="Hung parliament">hung parliament</a>. Six <a href="/wiki/Crossbencher" title="Crossbencher">crossbench</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Member_of_Parliament" title="Member of Parliament">MPs</a> shared the <a href="/wiki/Balance_of_power_(parliament)" title="Balance of power (parliament)">balance of power</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> The Greens signed a formal agreement with the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Labor_Party" title="Australian Labor Party">Australian Labor Party</a> involving consultation in relation to policy and support in the House of Representatives in relation to <a href="/wiki/Confidence_and_supply" title="Confidence and supply">confidence and supply</a> and three of the independents declared their support for Labor on confidence and supply,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3005179abc_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-3005179abc-47">[47]</a></sup> allowing Gillard and Labor to remain in power with a 76–74 minority government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_state_election,_2010" title="Victorian state election, 2010">2010 Victorian state election</a>, the Liberal party directed voters to preference the ALP ahead of the Greens. The Greens' primary vote increased slightly overall from 10.04% to 10.6% of the overall vote, but the party did not win any lower-house seats. Federal Greens leader <a href="/wiki/Bob_Brown" title="Bob Brown">Bob Brown</a> said of the result that it was positive but that: "The Liberals' preferencing to Labor means that instead of there being three Greens in the new parliament there won't be".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 24 February 2011, in a joint press conference of the "Climate Change Committee" – comprising the Government, Greens and two independent MPs – Prime Minister Gillard announced a plan to legislate for the introduction of a fixed price to be imposed on "carbon pollution" from 1 July 2012<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_tax_in_Australia" title="Carbon tax in Australia">carbon price</a> would be placed for three to five years before a full emissions trading scheme is implemented, under a blueprint agreed by a multi-party parliamentary committee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Key issues remained to be negotiated between the Government and the cross-benches, including compensation arrangements for households and businesses, the carbon price level, the emissions reduction target and whether or not to include fuel in the price.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Greens support protecting the welfare of the people of Libya and so supported the United States-led <a href="/wiki/2011_military_intervention_in_Libya" title="2011 military intervention in Libya">military intervention in Libya</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The view of Deputy leader <a href="/wiki/Christine_Milne" title="Christine Milne">Christine Milne</a>, that the Greens "want to make sure that [they] protect civilians wherever [they] can... to ensur[e] that we will save lives...", is commensurate with this position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greens_NSW" title="Greens NSW">Greens NSW</a> State Conference, which was held prior to the <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales_state_election,_2011" title="New South Wales state election, 2011">New South Wales state election in 2011</a>, a resolution was adopted in support for the <a href="/wiki/Boycott,_Divestment_and_Sanctions" title="Boycott, Divestment and Sanctions">Boycott, Divestment and Sanctions</a> campaign against <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AJN_BDS_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-AJN_BDS-55">[55]</a></sup> The move, proposed by <a href="/wiki/Sylvia_Hale" title="Sylvia Hale">Sylvia Hale</a> and backed by <a href="/wiki/Lee_Rhiannon" title="Lee Rhiannon">Lee Rhiannon</a>, had already been rejected by Leader <a href="/wiki/Bob_Brown" title="Bob Brown">Bob Brown</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-themonthly.com.au_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-themonthly.com.au-3">[3]</a></sup> Soon after, however, their motion was backed by the <a href="/wiki/Marrickville_Council" title="Marrickville Council">Marrickville Council</a> – resulting in a "boycott [against] all goods made in Israel and any sporting, academic, government or cultural exchanges".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Local Labor MP <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Albanese" title="Anthony Albanese">Anthony Albanese</a> called the move "misguided", sparking media interest and inciting anger among many Jewish Australians. The move also caused a rift within the Greens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-themonthly.com.au_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-themonthly.com.au-3">[3]</a></sup> Following the 2010 election, Bob Brown said that he had conveyed his disapproval of this policy to Rhiannon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Massola,_James_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Massola,_James-57">[57]</a></sup> Brown said that the policy was "a mistake" made by the NSW branch whereas Rhiannon said it had not been prosecuted hard enough.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-themonthly.com.au_3-8"><a href="#cite_note-themonthly.com.au-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Amidst ongoing debate over taxation, industry policy and climate change, Leader Bob Brown began to refer to sections within the Australian media that expressed criticism of Greens' policies or candidates as the "hate media", singling out the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/News_Limited" title="News Limited">Murdoch Press</a> in particular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Outlining his industry and climate policies on ABC's 7:30 Program in May 2011, Bob Brown voiced support for a reduction in subsidies to fossil fuel industries, the implementation of a price on carbon; a higher level of profit tax on the mining industry and a phasing out of Australia's coal export industry, saying: "The world is going to do that because it is causing massive economic damage down the line through the impact of climate change."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2011, the Greens called for the permanent closure of Australia's live export meat industry, following revelations of mistreatment of Australian cattle in some Indonesian abattoirs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 24 March <a href="/wiki/Queensland_state_election,_2012" title="Queensland state election, 2012">Queensland state election, 2012</a> the total primary vote for The <a href="/wiki/Queensland_Greens" title="Queensland Greens">Queensland Greens</a> fell by 0.84% to 7.53%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leadership_of_Christine_Milne">Leadership of Christine Milne</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Leadership of Christine Milne">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Frontbench_of_Christine_Milne" title="Frontbench of Christine Milne">Frontbench of Christine Milne</a></div>
<p>On 13 April 2012, Bob Brown announced that he was resigning as federal parliamentary leader of the Australian Greens and that he would leave the Senate in June. Christine Milne was elected unanimously as the new leader by the party. MP <a href="/wiki/Adam_Bandt" title="Adam Bandt">Adam Bandt</a> was elected deputy leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-resignation_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-resignation-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2014-09-21_Christine_Milne_Peoples_Climate_March_Melbourne_600_0479.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4016" data-file-width="6016" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/2014-09-21_Christine_Milne_Peoples_Climate_March_Melbourne_600_0479.JPG/220px-2014-09-21_Christine_Milne_Peoples_Climate_March_Melbourne_600_0479.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/2014-09-21_Christine_Milne_Peoples_Climate_March_Melbourne_600_0479.JPG/330px-2014-09-21_Christine_Milne_Peoples_Climate_March_Melbourne_600_0479.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/2014-09-21_Christine_Milne_Peoples_Climate_March_Melbourne_600_0479.JPG/440px-2014-09-21_Christine_Milne_Peoples_Climate_March_Melbourne_600_0479.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2014-09-21_Christine_Milne_Peoples_Climate_March_Melbourne_600_0479.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Christine Milne speaking at the Peoples Climate March in Melbourne in September 2014</div></div></div>
<p>The "ease of the Greens leadership transition" was widely praised,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> with one commentator noting "She has survived the transition in leadership with grace and steadfastness of vision"
,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> and Milne set about expanding the party's reach, looking first to regional Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smh.com.au_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-smh.com.au-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Milne took the leadership at a time when the Greens nationally had passed a peak. In the 18 months between the high water mark of the 2010 Federal Election and Brown's retirement, polls nationally were trending downwards. This was reflected in a number of setbacks for state and local Greens parties, which some commentators blamed on Brown's absence. The outcome of 20 October 2012 election in the ACT resulted in a reduction of Greens Legislative Assembly members, from four to one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Western_Australian_state_election,_2013" title="Western Australian state election, 2013">Western Australian state election</a> was held in March 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> For the Legislative Assembly, the total primary vote for the Greens fell by 3.52% to 8.40%. In the Legislative Council the Greens' representation was reduced from four to two members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Even some impressive results which failed to deliver wins, such as the <a href="/wiki/Melbourne_state_by-election,_2012" title="Melbourne state by-election, 2012">2012 Melbourne state by-election</a>, where the Greens received the highest number of first preference votes but did not win the seat as some had expected, were used to attack Milne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VEC_Melbourne_2012_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-VEC_Melbourne_2012-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a 19 February address to the <a href="/wiki/National_Press_Club_(Australia)" title="National Press Club (Australia)">National Press Club</a> in Canberra, Christine Milne announced that the Federal Greens alliance with the Labor Party was "effectively over".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> In particular, Milne cited a failure by the <a href="/wiki/Gillard_Government" title="Gillard Government">Gillard Government</a> to redraft the <a href="/wiki/Minerals_Resource_Rent_Tax" title="Minerals Resource Rent Tax">mining tax</a> it had concluded prior to the 2010 Election as evidence that the government had "walked away" from its agreement with the Greens. Nevertheless, Milne promised to continue to guarantee confidence and supply to the Labor Government on the floor of Parliament, so as not to "advance the interests" of Opposition Leader <a href="/wiki/Tony_Abbott" title="Tony Abbott">Tony Abbott</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theaustralian.com.au_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-theaustralian.com.au-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Milne was aware that the period in balance of power would be electorally costly for the party, telling members that "You earn political capital in opposition and you spend it in power."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-74">[74]</a></sup>  With that in mind, and following the disappointing state results, Milne and the Australian Greens set as their goal for the 2013 election a clear target of retaining their existing seats and perhaps win one more Senate seat in Victoria, rather than to increase the vote nationally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smh.com.au_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-smh.com.au-66">[66]</a></sup> Despite a reduction in the vote, maintaining and slightly increasing parliamentary representation is exactly what Milne achieved.
</p><p>Christine Milne resigned as leader of the Australian Greens on 6 May 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABCMilneResigns_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-ABCMilneResigns-75">[75]</a></sup> Milne was replaced by Victorian senator Richard Di Natale, with Adam Bandt being replaced as deputy leader by Larissa Waters and Scott Ludlam at the same time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SBSDiNataleIn_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-SBSDiNataleIn-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leadership_of_Richard_Di_Natale">Leadership of Richard Di Natale</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Leadership of Richard Di Natale">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Richard_Di_Natale" title="Richard Di Natale">Richard Di Natale</a> became leader of the Australian Greens following <a href="/wiki/Christine_Milne" title="Christine Milne">Christine Milne</a>'s resignation, on 6 May 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>Under Richard Di Natale, the party has taken a much more pragmatic approach to policy and dealing with government legislation than under previous leaders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>The party voted in support for legislation that saw assets testing for age pensions reduced from $1M down to $800,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> The Greens also negotiated with the government and secured a tax disclosure threshold for big businesses earning more than $200M a year.
</p><p>In November 2016 the Greens voted with the <a href="/wiki/Turnbull_Government" title="Turnbull Government">Turnbull government</a> to levy a 15% tax on foreign backpackers on working holiday visas.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2013_federal_election_onward">2013 federal election onward</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 2013 federal election onward">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table style="float:right; padding:1px 3px; margin:0 0 0 8px; Background:#eee; Border:2px outset #aaa; border-radius:15px;">
<tbody><tr>
<td>
<p><b>Top 10 primary votes in <a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2013" title="Australian federal election, 2013">2013</a></b>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Division_of_Melbourne" title="Division of Melbourne">Melbourne (Vic)</a>: 42.6%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Division_of_Batman" title="Division of Batman">Batman (Vic)</a>: 26.4%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Division_of_Grayndler" title="Division of Grayndler">Grayndler (NSW)</a>: 23.0%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Division_of_Wills" title="Division of Wills">Wills (Vic)</a>: 22.2%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Division_of_Melbourne_Ports" title="Division of Melbourne Ports">Melbourne Ports (Vic)</a>: 20.2%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Division_of_Richmond" title="Division of Richmond">Richmond (NSW)</a>: 17.7%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Division_of_Sydney" title="Division of Sydney">Sydney (NSW)</a>: 17.3%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Division_of_Higgins" title="Division of Higgins">Higgins (Vic)</a>: 16.8%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Division_of_Gellibrand" title="Division of Gellibrand">Gellibrand (Vic)</a>: 16.7%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Division_of_Kooyong" title="Division of Kooyong">Kooyong (Vic)</a>: 16.6%</li></ul>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2013" title="Australian federal election, 2013">2013 federal election</a> the <a href="/wiki/Australian_House_of_Representatives" title="Australian House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a> (lower house) primary vote was 8.7 percent (−3.1) with the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Senate" title="Australian Senate">Senate</a> (upper house) primary vote at 8.7 percent (−4.5). Despite that, as targeted, the Greens representation in the parliament increased. <a href="/wiki/Adam_Bandt" title="Adam Bandt">Adam Bandt</a> retained his <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Melbourne" title="Division of Melbourne">Melbourne</a> seat with a primary vote of 42.6 percent (+7.0) and a <a href="/wiki/Two-party-preferred_vote" title="Two-party-preferred vote">two-candidate preferred</a> vote of 55.3 percent (−0.6). The Greens won four Senate positions, increasing their Senate representation from nine to ten Senators to take effect from 1 July 2014, to a total of eleven Green members in the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Australia" title="Parliament of Australia">Parliament of Australia</a>.
</p><p>Writing in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Australian_Financial_Review" title="Australian Financial Review">Australian Financial Review</a>, Vincent Mahon, a former campaign manager for the Greens, said that while the Greens were able to present positive achievements to the electors relating to education, health, the environment and the promotion of clean energy, the party was unable to attract disenchanted Labor voters.  He noted that Green policies relating to carbon pricing and refugees were unpopular with many voters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Christine Milne said that the Greens support of the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Labor_Party" title="Australian Labor Party">Labor</a> minority government was a factor in the Greens' lower vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the federal election, South Australian Greens Senator, Sarah Hanson-Young, who had lost a ballot against Senator Milne for Deputy Leadership<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> and lost again to Adam Bandt, publicly criticised Senator Milne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2013, it was reported that six senior Greens' staffers had resigned including Chief of Staff, <a href="/wiki/Ben_Oquist" title="Ben Oquist">Ben Oquist</a>, who claimed there were, "fundamental differences of opinion on strategy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> There have been suggestions that Oquist was behind the unsuccessful attempt to create leadership tensions because he feared moves to "freeze him out".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Senate_special_election_in_Western_Australia,_2014" title="Australian Senate special election in Western Australia, 2014">2014 Western Australian Senate election</a> the Greens won in excess of a quota with the primary vote increasing from 9.5 to 15.6 percent, re-electing <a href="/wiki/Scott_Ludlam" title="Scott Ludlam">Scott Ludlam</a>. Ludlam threw his weight behind Milne's leadership, telling ABC radio on being asked if he had leadership ambitions that "That's very flattering, but Christine Milne is doing a great job".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 17 July 2015, Wright announced that she would be resigning from the Senate due to illness in her family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pwright_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-pwright-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>2016 federal election onward</b>
</p>
<table style="float:right; padding:1px 3px; margin:0 0 0 8px; Background:#eee; Border:2px outset #aaa; border-radius:15px;">
<tbody><tr>
<td>
<p><b>Top 10 primary votes in <a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2016" title="Australian federal election, 2016">2016</a></b>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Division_of_Melbourne" title="Division of Melbourne">Melbourne (Vic)</a>: 43.75%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Division_of_Batman" title="Division of Batman">Batman (Vic)</a>: 36.23%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Division_of_Wills" title="Division of Wills">Wills (Vic)</a>: 30.83%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Division_of_Higgins" title="Division of Higgins">Higgins (Vic)</a>: 25.33%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Division_of_Melbourne_Ports" title="Division of Melbourne Ports">Melbourne Ports (Vic)</a>: 23.79%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Division_of_Grayndler" title="Division of Grayndler">Grayndler (NSW)</a>: 22.24%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Division_of_Gellibrand" title="Division of Gellibrand">Gellibrand (Vic)</a>: 21.48%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Division_of_Richmond" title="Division of Richmond">Richmond (NSW)</a>: 20.44%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Division_of_Brisbane" title="Division of Brisbane">Brisbane (Qld)</a>: 19.4%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Division_of_Kooyong" title="Division of Kooyong">Kooyong (Vic)</a>: 18.92%</li></ul>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2016" title="Australian federal election, 2016">2016 federal election</a> the <a href="/wiki/Australian_House_of_Representatives" title="Australian House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a> (lower house) primary vote increased to 10.23 percent (+1.58) but decreased in the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Senate" title="Australian Senate">Senate</a> (upper house), with primary vote at 8.65 percent (−0.58). <a href="/wiki/Adam_Bandt" title="Adam Bandt">Adam Bandt</a> was elected to a third term in his <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Melbourne" title="Division of Melbourne">Melbourne</a> seat with a primary vote of 43.75 percent (+1.13) and a <a href="/wiki/Two-party-preferred_vote" title="Two-party-preferred vote">two-candidate preferred</a> vote of 68.48 percent (+13.21). Despite a campaign focus on winning additional seats in the lower house, The Greens failed to win any lower house contests. Their closest seats were <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Batman" title="Division of Batman">Batman</a>, where the Greens won the most first preference votes and turned the once safe Labor seat into a marginal Labor vs Green seat, and <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Melbourne_Ports" title="Division of Melbourne Ports">Melbourne Ports</a>, where the Greens were fewer than 1000 votes off overtaking Labor, which would have likely (but not certainly) resulted in a Greens victory. <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Wills" title="Division of Wills">Wills</a>, <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Higgins" title="Division of Higgins">Higgins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Grayndler" title="Division of Grayndler">Grayndler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Warringah" title="Division of Warringah">Division of Warringah</a> saw Greens make the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Two-candidate-preferred" title="Two-candidate-preferred">two-candidate-preferred</a> result. Deposits were retained in 141 of the 150 seats.
</p><p>The Greens also lost one Senate position in <a href="/wiki/South_Australia" title="South Australia">South Australia</a>, decreasing their Senate representation from ten to nine Senators, to a total of ten Green members in the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Australia" title="Parliament of Australia">Parliament of Australia</a>. The result was seen as disappointing, and caused internal divisions to flare up, with former Federal Leader Bob Brown calling upon Senator Lee Rhiannon to resign, citing the "need for renewal".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tensions within the party reached a flashpoint in late 2016 when a group of Greens members announced their intention to form a faction within the party called "<a href="/wiki/Left_Renewal" title="Left Renewal">Left Renewal</a>". Their stated aim is to "end capitalism" and stop "global imperialism". The faction's existence is supported by Senator Lee Rhiannon and State MP David Shoebridge, however party leader Richard Di Natale has publicly criticised the grouping saying its manifesto was "ridiculous" and its members should consider leaving the Greens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Former leader Bob Brown also attacking the group calling it "a joke".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>In June 2017 <a href="/wiki/Lee_Rhiannon" title="Lee Rhiannon">Lee Rhiannon</a> was suspended from the Federal Greens party room following an internal dispute over her opposition to the Federal Greens' support for the Turnbull government education funding changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Greens_New_South_Wales" title="Greens New South Wales">Greens New South Wales</a> subsequently issued a statement reiterating its support for Senator Rhiannon and support for public education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 14 July 2017, Ludlam resigned from the Senate after he <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2017_Australian_constitutional_crisis" title="2017 Australian constitutional crisis">found that he had dual Australian-New Zealand citizenship</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ludlam_resignation_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ludlam_resignation-92">[92]</a></sup> The next week on 18 July 2017, his former co-deputy, and Queensland Senator, <a href="/wiki/Larissa_Waters" title="Larissa Waters">Larissa Waters</a> resigned, after discovering that she held dual citizenship with <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, and that had she been born a week later that would not have been the case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Both were ineligible to be elected to Parliament under <a href="/wiki/Section_44_of_the_Constitution_of_Australia#(i)_Allegiance_to_a_foreign_power" title="Section 44 of the Constitution of Australia">section 44 of the Australian Constitution</a>. Subsequently, Adam Bandt and Rachel Siewert were named as temporary co-deputy leaders until the arrival of Ludlam and Waters' replacements in Canberra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ideology">Ideology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Ideology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Senator_Richard_Di_Natale_accepts_Fossil_of_the_Day_Award_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="1346" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Senator_Richard_Di_Natale_accepts_Fossil_of_the_Day_Award_01.jpg/170px-Senator_Richard_Di_Natale_accepts_Fossil_of_the_Day_Award_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Senator_Richard_Di_Natale_accepts_Fossil_of_the_Day_Award_01.jpg/255px-Senator_Richard_Di_Natale_accepts_Fossil_of_the_Day_Award_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Senator_Richard_Di_Natale_accepts_Fossil_of_the_Day_Award_01.jpg/340px-Senator_Richard_Di_Natale_accepts_Fossil_of_the_Day_Award_01.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Senator_Richard_Di_Natale_accepts_Fossil_of_the_Day_Award_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Richard_Di_Natale" title="Richard Di Natale">Richard Di Natale</a>, Green leader 2015–present</div></div></div>
<p>The Australian Greens are part of the global "<a href="/wiki/Green_politics" title="Green politics">green politics</a>" movement. The charter of the Australian Greens identifies the following as the four pillars of the party's policy: "<a href="/wiki/Social_justice" title="Social justice">social justice</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainability</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Grassroots_democracy" title="Grassroots democracy">grassroots democracy</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Peace" title="Peace">peace</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-violence" title="Non-violence">non-violence</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Major policy initiatives of recent years have also included taxation reform, review of the <a href="/wiki/ANZUS" title="ANZUS">American alliance</a>, and implementation of harm minimisation in relation to drug use.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Policy_positions">Policy positions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Policy positions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Animals">Animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Greens oppose the importation of animals for <a href="/wiki/Zoo" title="Zoo">zoos</a> in Australia, "except where the importation will assist the overall conservation of the species".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Animals_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Animals-97">[97]</a></sup>
They also seek to ban and phase out respectively the display of wild or domesticated animals in <a href="/wiki/Circus" title="Circus">circuses</a> in Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Animals_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-Animals-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Greens are also in favour of phasing out live animal exports,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> with Greens parliamentary leader Richard Di Natale instead favouring investment in the chilled meat industry:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>“Often what we’re told is that if you phase out the wrong the live animal export trade then we’re going to lose a whole lot of jobs. But what’s not being told is that if we started to invest in a frozen and chilled meat trade through northern Australia, then we could turn this issue into one where we value-add, one where create lots of jobs for Australians."
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_and_resources">Energy and resources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Energy and resources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Greens support the mass-rollout of <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a>, with an aim of 90% renewable energy production by 2030, and phasing out the use of coal-fired power, as a means of driving investment and creating jobs, which forms a basis of their "Renew Australia" plan that the party took to the 2016 Federal Election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>The party also opposes the opening of new coal mines, and has campaigned against the <a href="/wiki/Adani_Group" title="Adani Group">Adani</a> <a href="/wiki/Carmichael_coal_mine" title="Carmichael coal mine">Carmichael coal mine</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Abbot_Point" title="Abbot Point">Abbot Point</a> coal terminal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> The party calls for the phasing out, closure, and rehabilitation of currently operating coal mines; and the putting in place a transition plan for workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>The party is strongly opposed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fracking" title="Fracking">coal-seam gas mining</a> (fracking) and regularly participate and have even organised community demonstrations against the practice.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrastructure_and_transport">Infrastructure and transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Infrastructure and transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Greens are strongly in favour of expanding public transport, and support the building of a high speed rail network between <a href="/wiki/Sydney" title="Sydney">Sydney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>They also call for the establishment of an infrastructure bank, with the aim of increasing federal infrastructure investment to $75 billion as a means of funding, among other things, public transport, public housing, and maintaining existing infrastructure including local government roads and storm water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Greens support the construction of the national broadband network, as initially envisioned by the Rudd Government, which would increase <a href="/wiki/Optical_fiber" title="Optical fiber">fibre optic</a> home connections.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy">Foreign policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Foreign policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>Abolition of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organisation" title="World Trade Organisation">World Trade Organisation</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a> and <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> unless they can be democratised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-globaleconomics_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-globaleconomics-105">[105]</a></sup></li>
<li>Ending <a href="/wiki/ANZUS" title="ANZUS">Australia's Defence Treaty with the United States</a> unless it can be changed to operate within the Greens view on Australia's national interest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-couriermail1_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-couriermail1-106">[106]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1991, opposition to the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a>, and in 2003, the <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq War</a>; and continued opposition to the <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001–present)">Afghanistan War</a>.</li>
<li>Support for independence movements around the world, including in <a href="/wiki/State_of_Palestine" title="State of Palestine">Palestine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Tibet, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Papua_(region)" title="West Papua (region)">West Papua</a>.</li>
<li>In 1999, support for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Force_for_East_Timor" title="International Force for East Timor">armed intervention in East Timor</a>.</li>
<li>Support for human rights in countries such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a>.</li>
<li>Support for the <a href="/wiki/2011_military_intervention_in_Libya" title="2011 military intervention in Libya">2011 military intervention in Libya</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unagainstgaddafi_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-unagainstgaddafi-108">[108]</a></sup></li></ul>
