<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bioethics_and_family_policy">Bioethics and family policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Bioethics and family policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Greens are in support of voluntary <a href="/wiki/Euthanasia" title="Euthanasia">euthanasia</a> for those who are terminally ill.
</p><p>The Greens also support <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">same-sex marriage</a>, and often use the slogan "every vote, every time" in support. Same sex marriage was made legal in Australia in 2018.
</p><p>The party also supports <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_rights" title="Reproductive rights">reproductive rights</a> and expanding abortion services and making access to them easier.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxation">Taxation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Taxation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2010" title="Australian federal election, 2010">2010 federal election</a>: advocated an increase in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Company_tax" title="Company tax">company tax</a> rate to 33% and an increase in the <a href="/wiki/Gillard_Government" title="Gillard Government">Gillard Government</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mineral_Resource_Rent_Tax" title="Mineral Resource Rent Tax">Mineral Resource Rent Tax</a>; a new top marginal tax rate of 50%; the reintroduction of <a href="/wiki/Inheritance_tax" title="Inheritance tax">estate duties</a>; a "<a href="/wiki/Tobin_tax" title="Tobin tax">Tobin tax</a>" on foreign currency transactions; that family trusts be taxed as "companies"; the introduction of road congestion charges; and elimination of fringe benefit tax concessions for cars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-couriermail1_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-couriermail1-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theaustralian1_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-theaustralian1-109">[109]</a></sup></li>
<li>Estate duties were removed from the Australian Greens policy platform in November 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_1998" title="Australian federal election, 1998">1998 federal election</a>: opposition to the introduction of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goods_and_Services_Tax_(Australia)" title="Goods and Services Tax (Australia)">Goods &amp; Services Tax</a> (GST) (during the <a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2001" title="Australian federal election, 2001">2010 federal election</a> indicated that they would oppose the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Labor_Party" title="Australian Labor Party">Labor Party</a> proposal to remove the GST from gas and electricity bills).</li>
<li>Support the abolition of the 30% private health care rebate, so as to increase funding for public health care.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Immigration">Immigration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Immigration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The party is very vocal in speaking in favour of the rights of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asylum_seekers" title="Asylum seekers">asylum seekers</a>, and to this end support the abolishing of indefinite offshore detention of asylum seekers, as well as the practice of asylum seeker boat turn backs.
</p><p>In the 2016 Federal Election, the Greens proposed a one-time intake of 50,000 refugees from Syrian refugee camps, contrasting with the Coalition who proposed an intake of 12,000 and Labor who proposed an intake of 30,000.
</p><p>The Greens favour establishing a migrant program that prioritises family reunion and facilitates migration or resettlement to Australia within a reasonable time.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Law_reform">Law reform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Law reform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>Review relationship between the exclusive ownership of property and exclusive use of its resources.</li></ul>
<p>The Greens support the implementation of the Portuguese model when it comes to drug law reform - specifically, the decriminalisation of all drugs and treating drug addiction as a health issue rather than a law and order issue. Their approach to drug legalisation is on a case by case basis. They support legalising cannabis for medical and recreational use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GREENS_ANNOUNCE_NATIONAL_PLAN_TO_LEGALISE_CANNABIS_FOR_ADULT_USE_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-GREENS_ANNOUNCE_NATIONAL_PLAN_TO_LEGALISE_CANNABIS_FOR_ADULT_USE-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Indigenous_affairs">Indigenous affairs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Indigenous affairs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>Supported <a href="/wiki/Stolen_Generations" title="Stolen Generations">National Apology to the Stolen Generations</a>.</li>
<li>Opposed <a href="/wiki/Northern_Territory_National_Emergency_Response" title="Northern Territory National Emergency Response">Northern Territory National Emergency Response</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></li>
<li>Support the Queensland Wild Rivers legislation (a <a href="/wiki/Cape_York_Peninsula" title="Cape York Peninsula">Cape York</a> conservation initiative, opposed by <a href="/wiki/Noel_Pearson" title="Noel Pearson">Noel Pearson</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="LGBTIQ_rights">LGBTIQ rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: LGBTIQ rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>At the 2016 federal election, the Greens announced a policy to strengthen anti-discrimination laws, achieve <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">same-sex marriage</a>, stand up for the rights of <a href="/wiki/Intersex" title="Intersex">intersex</a> people, defend <a href="/wiki/Safe_Schools_Coalition_Australia" title="Safe Schools Coalition Australia">Safe Schools</a>, provide better access to HIV-prevention medication known as <a href="/wiki/Pre-exposure_prophylaxis" title="Pre-exposure prophylaxis">PrEP</a>, changing retrograde state laws including on <a href="/wiki/LGBT_adoption_and_parenting_in_Australia" title="LGBT adoption and parenting in Australia">adoption rights</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gay_panic_defense" title="Gay panic defense">'gay panic' defence</a>, providing easier access to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hormone_replacement_therapy_(transgender)" title="Hormone replacement therapy (transgender)">hormone treatments</a> for transgender and gender diverse young people, bring refugees being processed in countries which criminalise homosexuality to Australia, improving mental health for LGBTIQ people, providing training for LGBTIQ inclusive aged care services, removing the <a href="/wiki/Men_who_have_sex_with_men_blood_donor_controversy" title="Men who have sex with men blood donor controversy">gay blood ban</a>, and lifting <a href="/wiki/Aid" title="Aid">aid</a> funding including for HIV prevention projects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>A convenor of the <a href="/wiki/ACT_Greens" title="ACT Greens">ACT Greens</a> party has said when <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">same-sex marriage</a> is limited to, "two consenting adults [this] discriminates against others in the gay community, including <a href="/wiki/Polyamory" title="Polyamory">polyamorists</a>". He accused the Greens of being hypocrites because the logic they use to argue for marriage equality should extend to people who have <a href="/wiki/Group_marriage" title="Group marriage">multiple partners</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_Policy">Economic Policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Economic Policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Greens leader Richard Di Natale, in a speech delivered to the <a href="/wiki/National_Press_Club_(Australia)" title="National Press Club (Australia)">National Press Club</a> in March 2017, proposed that a discussion be had around the future of work in Australia, arguing that reduced work hours, or a shorter working week, will increase productivity and create new employment opportunities for those wishing to work more hours, while also allowing those wishing to work less the opportunity to do so. The shortfall in terms of wages earned would be made up through reform in the social welfare system; specifically, by the implementation of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_basic_income" title="Universal basic income">universal basic income</a>.
</p><p>The party also supports winding back and reforming <a href="/wiki/Negative_gearing" title="Negative gearing">negative gearing</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Stamp_duty" title="Stamp duty">stamp duty</a>, as a way of reducing the cost of housing.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Federal_leaders">Federal leaders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Federal leaders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On Saturday 12 November 2005 at the national conference in <a href="/wiki/Hobart" title="Hobart">Hobart</a> the Australian Greens abandoned their long-standing tradition of having no official leader and approved a process whereby a parliamentary leader could be elected by the Greens Parliamentary Party Room. On Monday 28 November 2005, Bob Brown – who had long been regarded as <i>de facto</i> leader by many inside the party, and most people outside the party – was elected unopposed as the Parliamentary Party Leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_Council">National Council</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: National Council">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Australian Greens, like all Australian political parties, is <a href="/wiki/Federalism" title="Federalism">federally</a> organised with separately registered state parties signing up to a national constitution, yet retaining considerable policy-making and organisational autonomy from the centre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> The national decision-making body of the Australian Greens is the National Council, consisting of delegates from each member body (a state or territory Greens party) and composed of national office bearers including the National Convenor, Secretary and Treasurer. There is also a Public Officer, a Party Agent and a Registered Officer. The National Council arrives at decisions by consensus. All policies originating from this structure are subject to ratification by the members of the Australian Greens at National Conference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="State_and_territory_parties">State and territory parties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: State and territory parties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The various <a href="/wiki/States_and_territories_of_Australia" title="States and territories of Australia">Australian states and territories</a> have different <a href="/wiki/Electoral_systems_of_the_Australian_states_and_territories" title="Electoral systems of the Australian states and territories">electoral systems</a>, all of which allow the Greens to gain representation. In New South Wales, Victoria, Western Australia and South Australia, the Greens hold seats in the Legislative Councils (upper houses), which are elected by <a href="/wiki/Proportional_representation" title="Proportional representation">proportional representation</a>. The Greens also hold two seats in the unicameral <a href="/wiki/Australian_Capital_Territory_Legislative_Assembly" title="Australian Capital Territory Legislative Assembly">Australian Capital Territory Legislative Assembly</a> since the 2016 election, up from one after the 2012 election. In Queensland and the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Territory" title="Northern Territory">Northern Territory</a>, their unicameral parliaments have made it difficult for the Greens to gain representation.
</p><p>The Australian Greens are a federation consisting of eight parties from each state and territory:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Australian_Greens_Victoria" title="Australian Greens Victoria">Australian Greens Victoria</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Greens_New_South_Wales" title="Greens New South Wales">Greens New South Wales</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Queensland_Greens" title="Queensland Greens">Queensland Greens</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Greens_Western_Australia" title="Greens Western Australia">Greens Western Australia</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Greens_South_Australia" title="Greens South Australia">Greens South Australia</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tasmanian_Greens" title="Tasmanian Greens">Tasmanian Greens</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/ACT_Greens" title="ACT Greens">ACT Greens</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Northern_Territory_Greens" title="Northern Territory Greens">Northern Territory Greens</a></li></ul>
<p>The Greens' most important area of state political activity has been in Tasmania, which is the only state where the lower house of the state parliament is elected by proportional representation. In Tasmania, the Greens have been represented in the <a href="/wiki/Tasmanian_House_of_Assembly" title="Tasmanian House of Assembly">House of Assembly</a> from 1983, initially as Green Independents, and from the early 1990s as an established party. At the <a href="/wiki/Tasmanian_state_election,_1989" title="Tasmanian state election, 1989">1989 state election</a>, the Liberal Party won 17 seats to Labor's 13 and the Greens' 5. The Greens agreed to support a minority Labor government in exchange for a number of policy commitments. In 1992 the agreement broke down over the issue of employment in the forestry industry, and the premier, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Michael_Field_(Australian_politician)" title="Michael Field (Australian politician)">Michael Field</a>, called an early state election which the Liberals won. Later, Labor and the Liberals combined to reduce the size of the Assembly from 35 to 25, thus raising the quota for election. At the <a href="/wiki/Tasmanian_state_election,_1998" title="Tasmanian state election, 1998">1998 election</a> the Greens won only one seat, despite their vote only falling slightly, mainly due to the new electoral system. They recovered in the <a href="/wiki/Tasmanian_state_election,_2002" title="Tasmanian state election, 2002">2002 election</a> when they won four seats. All four seats were retained in the <a href="/wiki/Tasmanian_state_election,_2006" title="Tasmanian state election, 2006">2006 election</a>. After gaining 5 seats in the <a href="/wiki/Tasmanian_state_election,_2010" title="Tasmanian state election, 2010">2010 election</a>, in April 2010 <a href="/wiki/Nick_McKim" title="Nick McKim">Nick McKim</a> became the first Green Minister in Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales_state_election,_2011" title="New South Wales state election, 2011">2011 NSW State election</a>, the Greens claimed their first lower-house seat in the district of <a href="/wiki/Electoral_district_of_Balmain" title="Electoral district of Balmain">Balmain</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_state_election,_2014" title="Victorian state election, 2014">2014 Victorian election</a>, they won two lower-house seats, those of <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prahran" title="Prahran">Prahran</a>.
</p><p>Three Greens have become ministers at the state/territory level: <a href="/wiki/Nick_McKim" title="Nick McKim">Nick McKim</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cassy_O%27Connor" title="Cassy O'Connor">Cassy O'Connor</a> in Tasmania until 2014, and <a href="/wiki/Shane_Rattenbury" title="Shane Rattenbury">Shane Rattenbury</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Capital_Territory" title="Australian Capital Territory">ACT</a> to the present.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Working_groups">Working groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Working groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A variety of working groups have been established by the National Council, which are directly accessible to all Greens members. Working groups perform an advisory function by developing policy, reviewing or developing the party structure, or by performing other tasks assigned by the National Council.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Young_Greens">Young Greens</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Young Greens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Australian_Young_Greens" title="Australian Young Greens">Australian Young Greens</a> are a federation of Young Greens groups from each Australian state and territory. Together they form the Youth Wing of the Australian Greens
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="LGBTIQ_Greens">LGBTIQ Greens</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: LGBTIQ Greens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A national Sexuality and Gender Identity Working Group existed from 2008-2012. It was concerned with advancing the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Greens#LGBTIQ_rights" title="Australian Greens">party's position on LGBTIQ rights</a>. The last National Conference agreed to start planning to revive the group.
</p><p>There are LGBTIQ working groups in some state and territory parties, including:
</p>
<ul><li><a class="external text" href="http://greens.org.au/vic/queergreens" rel="nofollow">Queer Greens Victoria</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Queensland_Greens#Other_working_groups" title="Queensland Greens">Queensland Rainbow Greens</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SA_Greens#Member_action_groups" title="SA Greens">SA Greens Queer Members Action Group</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Greens_New_South_Wales#Working_groups" title="Greens New South Wales">NSW Greens Sex, Sexuality and Gender Identity Working Group</a></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interactions_with_other_political_groups">Interactions with other political groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Interactions with other political groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Greens do not have formal links to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_organisation" title="Environmental organisation">environmental organisations</a> commonly labelled by the media as "green groups" such as the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Conservation_Foundation" title="Australian Conservation Foundation">Australian Conservation Foundation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tasmanian_Wilderness_Society" title="Tasmanian Wilderness Society">The Wilderness Society</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greenpeace" title="Greenpeace">Greenpeace</a>, all of whom claim to be non-partisan. However, it is common for the media to report the activities of such groups and those of The Greens under the general category of "greens". During elections, there is sometimes competition between The Greens and one or more of these groups negotiating "greens preferences" with other parties. The Greens preference negotiation objectives are to attempt to get Greens senators elected, and to get policy outcomes on issues like Tasmanian forests, though these objectives may be to a greater or lesser extent in conflict and the Greens more often direct preferences to Labor than the Liberals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> but it is claimed that this did not affect federal election outcomes in 2001 and 2004.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Labor_Party_and_unions">Labor Party and unions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Labor Party and unions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Greens were in a formal alliance with the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Labor_Party" title="Australian Labor Party">Australian Labor Party</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tasmanian_Parliament" title="Tasmanian Parliament">Tasmanian Parliament</a> under the <a href="/wiki/David_Bartlett" title="David Bartlett">Bartlett</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lara_Giddings" title="Lara Giddings">Giddings</a> governments between 2010 and 2014, and signed a formal agreement with the minority <a href="/wiki/Gillard_Government" title="Gillard Government">Gillard Labor Government</a> in the Federal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Australian_Parliament" title="Australian Parliament">Australian Parliament</a> in 2010. Milne declared this agreement "effectively over" in February 2013, but said that the Greens would continue to support Labor in the Parliament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theaustralian.com.au_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-theaustralian.com.au-73">[73]</a></sup> Generally the Greens preference Labor ahead of the Coalition at elections.
</p><p>Many Labor supporters and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trade_unionists" title="Trade unionists">trade unionists</a> see the Greens' policies as destructive of employment in industries like mining and forestry. The forestry industry has been a particular target of environmental campaigns and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CFMEU#Forestry_and_Furnishing_Products_Division" title="CFMEU">Forestry Division of the CFMEU</a> have actively campaigned against the Greens. Left-wing trade unionists and some members of Labor's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socialist_Left_(Australia)" title="Socialist Left (Australia)">Left faction</a> sympathise with the Greens' social policies and often identify more readily with the Greens than with the <a href="/wiki/Labor_Right" title="Labor Right">Labor Right</a>. Some unionists, such as <a href="/wiki/National_Tertiary_Education_Union" title="National Tertiary Education Union">NTEU</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AMWU" title="AMWU">AMWU</a> members have run for State or Federal parliament for the Greens. South Australian Labor MP, <a href="/wiki/Kris_Hanna" title="Kris Hanna">Kris Hanna</a>, defected to the Australian Greens in 2003 (before leaving the Greens in 2006, and being re-elected as an independent in the <a href="/wiki/South_Australian_state_election,_2006" title="South Australian state election, 2006">2006 South Australian election</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> In 2008, Queensland Labor MP <a href="/wiki/Ronan_Lee" title="Ronan Lee">Ronan Lee</a> defected to the Greens, becoming the first-ever Greens MP in the unicameral Queensland parliament. He said he made the decision after the Queensland government had "failed to act" against climate change. In 2015, the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_Trades_Union_of_Australia" title="Electrical Trades Union of Australia">Electrical Trades Union of Australia</a> (ETU) invited federal Greens MP <a href="/wiki/Adam_Bandt" title="Adam Bandt">Adam Bandt</a> as a speaker at the ETU National Officers' Conference in <a href="/wiki/Adelaide" title="Adelaide">Adelaide</a> on account of their shared opposition to the China-Australia Free Trade Agreement, which was approved by both the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Labor_Party" title="Australian Labor Party">ALP</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_of_Australia" title="Liberal Party of Australia">LNP</a> .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-125">[125]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Adam_Bandt" title="Adam Bandt">Adam Bandt</a> also spoke of the importance of "transition funding" to support workers and communities who would be affected by a transition from coal-fired power to renewable energy sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-125">[125]</a></sup> The Greens also announced Jim Casey, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NSW" title="NSW">NSW</a> state secretary of the Fire Brigade Employees Union, as the candidate for the 2016 federal election in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grayndler" title="Grayndler">Grayndler</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, these Green sympathies are not universal within Labor's Left and the two groups often find themselves competing in elections, making the Greens' growing popularity a threat to Labor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> In 2002, Labor front bencher and prominent Left member <a href="/wiki/Lindsay_Tanner" title="Lindsay Tanner">Lindsay Tanner</a> wrote "The emergence of the Greens... is already hurting the ALP's ability to attract new members amongst young people."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> During the 2004 campaign, Tanner's own seat of <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Melbourne" title="Division of Melbourne">Melbourne</a> in Victoria was thought to be under serious threat by the Greens and he described Greens policies as "mad".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> In the end, Tanner held the seat comfortably on primary votes (51.78%, +4.35-point swing).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> He did not stand for election at the 2010 election and his seat was won by the Greens.
</p><p>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2006_Victorian_state_election" title="2006 Victorian state election">2006 Victorian state election</a>, there was increased bitterness between Labor and the Greens. Labor direct-mailed a letter from <a href="/wiki/Peter_Garrett" title="Peter Garrett">Peter Garrett</a> to voters in its threatened inner-Melbourne seats claiming that the Greens were preferencing the Liberal Party, in spite of Greens preferences being either for Labor or being open.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Following the election, The Age's Paul Austin wrote "Labor's campaign manager, state secretary Stephen Newnham, reckons he knows why the Greens' support fell away in the last days of the campaign. He has told cabinet and caucus members it was because of Labor's loud assertions that the Greens had done a secret preferences deal with the Liberals".
</p><p>In April 2007, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Age" title="The Age">The Age</a></i> reported<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> that the Victorian Greens had published a poem titled <i>The Battle of Jeff's Shed</i>, by Mike Puleston, describing ALP officials and volunteers who scrutinised vote counting after the state election as "the Labor Panzers and their hardened SS troops – SS stood for Sturm Scrutineers". The poem described the final vote count at the Melbourne Exhibition Centre, which finished about 4 am on 14 December and resulted in the election of three Greens MLCs. Labor directed preferences in the upper house to the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Labour_Party_(Australia)" title="Democratic Labour Party (Australia)">DLP</a> above the Greens, which resulted in their preferences indirectly electing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peter_Kavanagh_(Australian_politician)" title="Peter Kavanagh (Australian politician)">Peter Kavanagh</a> from DLP in <a href="/wiki/Western_Victoria_Region" title="Western Victoria Region">Western Victoria Region</a>.
</p><p>Prior to the 2010 Federal Election, the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_Trades_Union_of_Australia" title="Electrical Trades Union of Australia">Electrical Trades Union</a>'s Victorian branch donated $325,000 to the Greens' Victorian campaign – the largest political donation ever directed to the Party up to that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>In March 2011, division emerged within the Labor Party over Prime Minister Gillard's initial support for a Greens proposal to remove the commonwealth veto over Territory legislation. <a href="/wiki/Joe_de_Bruyn" title="Joe de Bruyn">Joe de Bruyn</a>, head of the Shop, Distributors and Allied Employees Association, said "Everybody in the federal parliament knows that this is simply a way of letting the territories into <a href="/wiki/Euthanasia" title="Euthanasia">euthanasia</a> or whatever else they want to do". Anti-euthanasia Labor senators called on Gillard to overturn Labor's support for the Greens plan and press reports said some Labor senators had complained that the issue had not been discussed in Cabinet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> Prime Minister Gillard said that no caucus members had raised concerns with her over the influence of the Greens over Labor policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> Amidst suggestions that Labor was "too close" to the Greens, Prime Minister Gillard said in March: "The Greens are not a party of government and have no tradition of striking the balance required to deliver major reform".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Coalition">The Coalition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: The Coalition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Relations between the Greens and the <a href="/wiki/Coalition_(Australia)" title="Coalition (Australia)">Liberal-National Coalition</a> are generally poor and the Greens usually direct voters to preference the Labor Party ahead of the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_of_Australia" title="Liberal Party of Australia">Liberals</a> or <a href="/wiki/National_Party_of_Australia" title="National Party of Australia">Nationals</a> in Australian elections. The Coalition has however directed strategic preferences to the Greens over Labor in the past, as in the <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Melbourne" title="Division of Melbourne">Division of Melbourne</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Adam_Bandt" title="Adam Bandt">Adam Bandt</a> was elected at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2010_Australian_Federal_Election" title="2010 Australian Federal Election">2010 Australian Federal Election</a> with Liberal Preferences. In addition, the Tasmanian Liberal Party under <a href="/wiki/Tony_Rundle" title="Tony Rundle">Tony Rundle</a> managed to form a minority government through an informal alliance with the Tasmanian Greens between 1996 and 1998, enacting some progressive reforms in favour of forest conservation and LGBT rights during its term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> At the 2010 Victorian State Election, the Liberals put their preference for the Greens below the Labor Party.
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2004" title="Australian federal election, 2004">2004 federal election</a> the Australian Greens were branded as "environmental extremists" and "fascists" by some members of the Liberal-National Coalition Government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Anderson_(Australian_politician)" title="John Anderson (Australian politician)">John Anderson</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> described the Greens as 'watermelons', being "green on the outside and red on the inside". <a href="/wiki/John_Howard" title="John Howard">John Howard</a>, while Australian Prime Minister and leader of the Liberal Party, stated that "The Greens are not just about the environment. They have a whole lot of other very, very kooky policies in relation to things like drugs and all of that sort of stuff".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>Former Federal Conservation Minister <a href="/wiki/Eric_Abetz" title="Eric Abetz">Eric Abetz</a> criticised former Australian Greens senators <a href="/wiki/Bob_Brown" title="Bob Brown">Bob Brown</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kerry_Nettle" title="Kerry Nettle">Kerry Nettle</a> for spending most of their time on non-environmental issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2011, Liberal Shadow Cabinet frontbencher <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kevin_Andrews_(Australian_politician)" title="Kevin Andrews (Australian politician)">Kevin Andrews</a> published a critique of the Greens policy agenda for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quadrant_Magazine" title="Quadrant Magazine">Quadrant Magazine</a> in which he wrote that the Greens' "objective involves a radical transformation of the culture that underpins Western civilisation" and that their agenda would threaten the "<a href="/wiki/Judeo-Christian" title="Judeo-Christian">Judeo-Christian</a>/<a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> synthesis that upholds the individual" as well as "the economic system that has resulted in the creation of wealth and prosperity for the most people in human history."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2013, Liberal Party Treasurer <a href="/wiki/Joe_Hockey" title="Joe Hockey">Joe Hockey</a> secured a deal with the Greens to remove the debt ceiling in response to debt approaching the current limit of $300b, despite opposition from the Labor Party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> In December 2015, the Greens struck a deal with the Coalition Government, passing a law requiring multinational private companies with a turnover over $200 million to disclose their tax arrangements and also making it mandatory for multinational companies with a <i>global</i> turnover of $1 billion or more to have to prepare "general purpose" financial statements, which disclose greater tax details than previously occurred in Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Once more the following year the Coalition Government and the Greens agreed on a permanent 15% tax rate for <a href="/wiki/Backpacking_(travel)" title="Backpacking (travel)">backpackers</a>, in exchange for a $100 million funding boost to environmental stewardship <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Not-for-profit" title="Not-for-profit">not-for-profit</a> Landcare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_minor_parties">Other minor parties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Other minor parties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In a similar vein to the <a href="/wiki/Family_First_Party" title="Family First Party">Family First</a> television advertisements in 2004, <a href="/wiki/Australian_Country_Party_(2004)" title="Australian Country Party (2004)">Country Alliance</a> also ran television advertisements<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> in the lead up to the 2006 Victorian state election claiming that the Greens policies were "extreme".
</p><p>The Greens have voiced opposition and even organised protests against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/One_Nation_Party" title="One Nation Party">One Nation Party</a> (an anti-immigration, economically protectionist Party which enjoyed significant publicity in the 1998 Federal Election).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Support_base">Support base</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Support base">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The support base of the Greens has generally consisted of those who have become disillusioned with the main parties, particularly their perceived rightward drift of the Australian Labor Party and its alleged abandonment of its traditional social democratic and environmental principles in preference to neoliberal ideology. As such, a large portion of the Greens support base are disillusioned Labor voters from its Left who feel abandoned by the party they once supported.
</p><p>The Greens also absorbed much of the Democrats support base following its downfall as the third party in Australia and many of the social and environmental policies and issues the Democrats advocated for have been taken up by the Greens. Much like the Democrats, the Greens have a higher proportion of supporters who are university educated, under 40, who identify as professionals in their field (teachers, graphic designers, engineers, etc. as well as small business owners) and who earn above the national average wage.
</p><p>Generally speaking, the areas of highest consistent support for the Greens tends to be in the gentrifying high-density inner city suburbs of the major cities, particularly in Melbourne, Sydney, Hobart and increasingly Brisbane. The Greens also poll relatively well in the most wealthy 'blue-ribbon' areas, such as in the inner northern suburbs of Sydney and the inner east of Melbourne, which consist of seats safely held by the Liberal Party. The Greens tend to poll poorly in the poorer working class mid and outer suburbs of the major cities.
</p><p>There is also a growing support for the Greens in some regional and rural areas, particularly in the northern coastal regions of New South Wales and the Surf Coast region of Victoria, around environmental and sustainability issues due to the Greens environmental principles, i.e. their opposition to coal-seam gas mining. However, by and large, Greens support in the most scarcely populated rural areas tends to be very low.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Former">Former</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Former">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>Senator <a href="/wiki/Jo_Vallentine" title="Jo Vallentine">Jo Vallentine</a>, 1990–1992, Greens WA (originally elected in 1984 as <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Disarmament_Party" title="Nuclear Disarmament Party">Nuclear Disarmament Party</a>)</li>
<li>Senator <a href="/wiki/Christabel_Chamarette" title="Christabel Chamarette">Christabel Chamarette</a>, 1992–1996, Greens WA</li>
<li>Senator <a href="/wiki/Dee_Margetts" title="Dee Margetts">Dee Margetts</a>, 1993–1999, Greens WA (defeated in 1998)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Michael_Organ" title="Michael Organ">Michael Organ</a> MP for <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Cunningham" title="Division of Cunningham">Cunningham</a> (NSW), 2002–2004</li>
<li>Senator <a href="/wiki/Kerry_Nettle" title="Kerry Nettle">Kerry Nettle</a> (NSW), 2002–2008 (elected in 2001, defeated in 2007)</li>
<li>Senator <a href="/wiki/Bob_Brown" title="Bob Brown">Bob Brown</a> (Tasmania), 1996–2012 (elected in 1996, resigned in 2012)</li>
<li>Senator <a href="/wiki/Christine_Milne" title="Christine Milne">Christine Milne</a> (Tasmania), 2005-2015 (elected in 2004, resigned in 2015)</li>
<li>Senator <a href="/wiki/Penny_Wright" title="Penny Wright">Penny Wright</a> (SA), 2011–2015 (elected in 2010, resigned in 2015)</li>
<li>Senator <a href="/wiki/Robert_Simms_(politician)" title="Robert Simms (politician)">Robert Simms</a> (SA), 2015–2016</li>
<li>Senator <a href="/wiki/Scott_Ludlam" title="Scott Ludlam">Scott Ludlam</a> (WA), 2008-2017 (elected in 2007, resigned and disqualified in 2017)</li>
<li>Senator <a href="/wiki/Lee_Rhiannon" title="Lee Rhiannon">Lee Rhiannon</a> (NSW), 2011-2018 (elected in 2010, resigned in 2018)</li>
<li>Senator <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Bartlett" title="Andrew Bartlett">Andrew Bartlett</a> (QLD), 2017–2018</li></ul>
<p>Senators Vallentine, Chamarette and Margetts were all elected as <a href="/wiki/Greens_Western_Australia" title="Greens Western Australia">Greens (WA)</a> senators and served their terms before the Greens WA affiliated to the Australian Greens, meaning that they were not considered to be Australian Greens senators at the time.
</p>

<p>For a full list of current and former Greens members of parliament in the <a href="/wiki/States_and_territories_of_Australia" title="States and territories of Australia">states and territories</a> see:<br/>
<b>List of Greens MPs in</b>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/ACT_Greens" title="ACT Greens">Australian Capital Territory</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Greens_New_South_Wales" title="Greens New South Wales">New South Wales</a>;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Queensland_Greens" title="Queensland Greens">Queensland</a>;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Greens_South_Australia" title="Greens South Australia">South Australia</a>;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tasmanian_Greens" title="Tasmanian Greens">Tasmania</a>;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Australian_Greens_Victoria" title="Australian Greens Victoria">Victoria</a>;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Greens_Western_Australia" title="Greens Western Australia">Western Australia</a>;</li></ul>
<p>Note that the Greens have never had any representation in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Territory_Greens" title="Northern Territory Greens">Northern Territory</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_notable_members">Other notable members</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Other notable members">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Andrew_Bartlett" title="Andrew Bartlett">Andrew Bartlett</a>, former <a href="/wiki/Australian_Democrats" title="Australian Democrats">Democrats</a> Leader</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Clive_Hamilton" title="Clive Hamilton">Clive Hamilton</a>, Greens candidate for the <a href="/wiki/Higgins_by-election,_2009" title="Higgins by-election, 2009">Higgins by-election, 2009</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chris_Harris_(New_South_Wales_politician)" title="Chris Harris (New South Wales politician)">Chris Harris</a>, Greens Councillor for the <a href="/wiki/City_of_Sydney" title="City of Sydney">City of Sydney</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jean_Jenkins_(politician)" title="Jean Jenkins (politician)">Jean Jenkins</a>, former <a href="/wiki/Australian_Democrats" title="Australian Democrats">Democrats</a> Senator for Western Australia</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kathleen_Maltzahn" title="Kathleen Maltzahn">Kathleen Maltzahn</a>, former Greens Councillor of the <a href="/wiki/City_of_Yarra" title="City of Yarra">City of Yarra</a> and candidate for the seat of Richmond at the 2010 and 2014 Victorian Elections</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Janet_Powell" title="Janet Powell">Janet Powell</a>, former <a href="/wiki/Australian_Democrats" title="Australian Democrats">Democrats</a> Leader.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Peter_Singer" title="Peter Singer">Peter Singer</a>, Greens candidate for the <a href="/wiki/Kooyong_by-election,_1994" title="Kooyong by-election, 1994">Kooyong by-election, 1994</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Brian_Walters" title="Brian Walters">Brian Walters</a> SC, prominent Human Rights lawyer and candidate for the state seat of Melbourne at the 2010 Victorian Election</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Andrew_Wilkie" title="Andrew Wilkie">Andrew Wilkie</a>, former Greens candidate and, as of  August 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, independent federal member for <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Denison" title="Division of Denison">Denison</a>, Tasmania</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Donors">Donors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Donors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Political_funding_in_Australia" title="Political funding in Australia">Political funding in Australia</a></div>
<p>For the 2015-2016 financial year, the top ten disclosed donors to the Greens Party were: <a href="/wiki/Graeme_Wood_(businessman)" title="Graeme Wood (businessman)">Graeme Wood (businessman)</a> ($500,000), Duncan Turpie ($400,000), <a href="/wiki/Electrical_Trades_Union_of_Australia" title="Electrical Trades Union of Australia">Electrical Trades Union of Australia</a> ($320,000), <a href="/wiki/Louise_Crossley" title="Louise Crossley">Louise Crossley</a> ($138,000), Anna Hackett ($100,000), Pater Investments ($100,000), Ruth Greble ($35,000), <a class="external text" href="https://www.aus61business.com/company/Minax-Uriel-Pty-Ltd" rel="nofollow">Minax Uriel Ptd Ltd</a> ($35,000) and <a href="/wiki/Chilla_Bulbeck" title="Chilla Bulbeck">Chilla Bulbeck</a> ($32,000).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Greens Party also receives undisclosed funding through several methods, such as "associated entities". The Green Institute Limited is an entity which has been used to funnel donations to the Greens Party without disclosing the source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[153]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Greens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-150"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-150">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Bob_Brown" title="Bob Brown">Bob Brown</a> was elected to the senate in 1996 as a representative of the <a href="/wiki/Tasmanian_Greens" title="Tasmanian Greens">Tasmanian Greens</a>. By the time of the 1998 election (where he was not up for re-election), the Tasmanian Greens had affiliated with the national organisation.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
